				<div class="minerva__subtitle"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs_Group&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Goldman Sachs Group">Goldman Sachs Group</a>)</span></div>

			<div id="bodyContent" class="content"><div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="mw-parser-output"><section class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0">
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<p><b>The Goldman Sachs Group, Inc.</b>, (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is an American multinational <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment bank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">financial services</a> company headquartered in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. It offers services in <a href="/wiki/Investment_management" title="Investment management">investment management</a>, securities, <a href="/wiki/Asset_management" title="Asset management">asset management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prime_brokerage" title="Prime brokerage">prime brokerage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Securities_underwriting" class="mw-redirect" title="Securities underwriting">securities underwriting</a>.
</p><table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><caption class="fn org">The Goldman Sachs Group, Inc.</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" class="logo" style="text-align:center"><a style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Number of employees</div></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><img alt="Increase" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/11px-Increase2.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Increase" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/17px-Increase2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/22px-Increase2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300"> 38,300 (2019)<sup id="cite_ref-AR19_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AR19-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AR18_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AR18-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Subsidiary" title="Subsidiary">Subsidiaries</a></th><td style="line-height: Ratings</a>: A</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Website</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://goldmansachs.com">goldmansachs<wbr></wbr>.com</a></span></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height: 1.35em;"><b>Footnotes / references</b><br><sup id="cite_ref-10Q18_earnings_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10Q18_earnings-3">[3]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table><p>The bank is one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_investment_banks" title="List of investment banks">largest investment banking enterprises</a> in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and is a <a href="/wiki/Primary_dealer" title="Primary dealer">primary dealer</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Treasury_security" title="United States Treasury security">United States Treasury security</a> market and more generally, a prominent <a href="/wiki/Market_maker" title="Market maker">market maker</a>. The group also owns Goldman Sachs Bank USA, a <a href="/wiki/Direct_bank" title="Direct bank">direct bank</a>. Goldman Sachs was founded in 1869 and is headquartered at <a href="/wiki/200_West_Street" title="200 West Street">200 West Street</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan" title="Lower Manhattan">Lower Manhattan</a> with additional offices in other international financial centers.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p><p>As a result of its involvement in securitization during the <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">subprime mortgage crisis</a>, Goldman Sachs suffered during the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2008">financial crisis of 2007–2008</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and received a $10 billion investment from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Department of the Treasury">United States Department of the Treasury</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">Troubled Asset Relief Program</a>, a financial <a href="/wiki/Bailout" title="Bailout">bailout</a> created by the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Economic_Stabilization_Act_of_2008" title="Emergency Economic Stabilization Act of 2008">Emergency Economic Stabilization Act of 2008</a>. The investment was made in November 2008 and was repaid in June 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2009June17_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2009June17-9">[9]</a></sup></p><p><a href="/wiki/List_of_former_employees_of_Goldman_Sachs" title="List of former employees of Goldman Sachs">Former employees of Goldman Sachs</a> have moved on to government positions. Notable examples includes former U.S. Secretaries of the Treasury <a href="/wiki/Robert_Rubin" title="Robert Rubin">Robert Rubin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Paulson" title="Henry Paulson">Henry Paulson</a>; current United States Secretary of the Treasury <a href="/wiki/Steven_Mnuchin" title="Steven Mnuchin">Steven Mnuchin</a>; former Under Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/John_C._Whitehead" title="John C. Whitehead">John C. Whitehead</a>; former chief economic advisor <a href="/wiki/Gary_Cohn_(investment_banker)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gary Cohn (investment banker)">Gary Cohn</a>; <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senator</a> and former <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_Jersey" title="Governor of New Jersey">Governor of New Jersey</a> <a href="/wiki/Jon_Corzine" title="Jon Corzine">Jon Corzine</a>; former Prime Minister of Italy <a href="/wiki/Mario_Monti" title="Mario Monti">Mario Monti</a>; European Central Bank President <a href="/wiki/Mario_Draghi" title="Mario Draghi">Mario Draghi</a>; former Bank of Canada Governor and current Governor of the Bank of England <a href="/wiki/Mark_Carney" title="Mark Carney">Mark Carney</a>; and the former Prime Minister of Australia <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Turnbull" title="Malcolm Turnbull">Malcolm Turnbull</a>. In addition, former Goldman employees have headed the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, and competing banks such as <a href="/wiki/Citigroup" title="Citigroup">Citigroup</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" class="mw-redirect" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch</a>.
</p><p>The company is ranked 62nd on the <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500">Fortune 500</a> list of the largest United States corporations by total revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_and_establishment">Founding and establishment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Founding and establishment" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Goldman%E2%80%93Sachs_family" title="Goldman–Sachs family">Goldman–Sachs family</a></div>
<p>Goldman Sachs was founded in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a> in 1869 by <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Goldman" title="Marcus Goldman">Marcus Goldman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In 1882, Goldman's son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Sachs" title="Samuel Sachs">Samuel Sachs</a> joined the firm.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In 1885, Goldman took his son Henry and his son-in-law Ludwig Dreyfuss into the business and the firm adopted its present name, Goldman Sachs &amp; Co.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The company pioneered the use of <a href="/wiki/Commercial_paper" title="Commercial paper">commercial paper</a> for entrepreneurs and joined the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a> (NYSE) in 1896.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup> By 1898, the firm's capital stood at $1.6 million.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup></p><p>Goldman entered the <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offering</a> market in 1906 when it took <a href="/wiki/Sears,_Roebuck_and_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Sears, Roebuck and Company">Sears, Roebuck and Company</a> public.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup> The deal was facilitated by Henry Goldman's personal friendship with  <a href="/wiki/Julius_Rosenwald" title="Julius Rosenwald">Julius Rosenwald</a>, an owner of Sears.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup> Other IPOs followed, including <a href="/wiki/F._W._Woolworth_Company" title="F. W. Woolworth Company">F. W. Woolworth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Continental_Can" class="mw-redirect" title="Continental Can">Continental Can</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup> In 1912, Henry S. Bowers became the first non-member of the founding family to become a partner of the company and share in its profits.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup></p><p>In 1917, under growing pressure from the other partners in the firm due to his pro-German stance, Henry Goldman resigned.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup> The Sachs family gained full control of the firm until <a href="/wiki/Waddill_Catchings" title="Waddill Catchings">Waddill Catchings</a> joined the company in 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup> By 1928, Catchings was the Goldman partner with the single largest stake in the firm.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup></p><p>On December 4, 1928, the firm launched the Goldman Sachs Trading Corp, a <a href="/wiki/Closed-end_fund" title="Closed-end fund">closed-end fund</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The fund failed during the <a href="/wiki/Stock_Market_Crash_of_1929" class="mw-redirect" title="Stock Market Crash of 1929">Stock Market Crash of 1929</a>, amid accusations that Goldman had engaged in share price manipulation and <a href="/wiki/Insider_trading" title="Insider trading">insider trading</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-20th_century">Mid-20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mid-20th century" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 1930, the firm ousted Catchings, and <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Weinberg" title="Sidney Weinberg">Sidney Weinberg</a> assumed the role of senior partner and shifted Goldman's focus away from trading and toward <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment banking</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup> Weinberg's actions helped to restore some of Goldman's tarnished reputation. Under Weinberg's leadership, Goldman was lead advisor on the <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford Motor Company</a>'s IPO in 1956, a major coup on Wall Street at the time. Under Weinberg's reign, the firm started an investment research division and a <a href="/wiki/Municipal_bond" title="Municipal bond">municipal bond</a> department, and it became an early innovator in <a href="/wiki/Risk_arbitrage" title="Risk arbitrage">risk arbitrage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup></p><p>In the 1950's, <a href="/wiki/Gus_Levy" title="Gus Levy">Gus Levy</a> joined the firm as a securities trader, where two powers fought for supremacy, one from investment banking and one from securities trading. Levy was a pioneer in <a href="/wiki/Block_trade" title="Block trade">block trading</a> and the firm established this trend under his guidance. Due to Weinberg's heavy influence, the firm formed an investment banking division in 1956 in an attempt to shift focus off Weinberg.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohan2012_15-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohan2012-15">[15]</a></sup></p><p>In 1969, Levy took over Weinberg's role as Senior Partner and built Goldman's trading franchise once again.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Levy is credited with Goldman's famous philosophy of being "long-term greedy," which implied that as long as money is made over the long term, short-term losses are bearable. At the same time, partners reinvested nearly all of their earnings in the firm.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> That same year, Weinberg retired from the firm.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>Another financial crisis for the firm occurred in 1970, when the <a href="/wiki/Penn_Central_Transportation_Company" title="Penn Central Transportation Company">Penn Central Transportation Company</a> went bankrupt with over $80 million in <a href="/wiki/Commercial_paper" title="Commercial paper">commercial paper</a> outstanding, most of it issued through Goldman Sachs. The bankruptcy was large, and the resulting lawsuits, notably by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">SEC</a>, threatened the partnership capital, life, and reputation of the firm.<sup id="cite_ref-cohan_wpost2012_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cohan_wpost2012-19">[19]</a></sup> It was this bankruptcy that resulted in <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating" title="Credit rating">credit ratings</a> for every issuer of commercial paper today by several credit rating services.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p><p>Under the direction of Senior Partner Stanley R. Miller, the firm opened its first international office in London in 1970 and created a <a href="/wiki/Private_Wealth_Management" class="mw-redirect" title="Private Wealth Management">private wealth</a> division along with a <a href="/wiki/Fixed_income" title="Fixed income">fixed income</a> division in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It pioneered the "<a href="/wiki/White_knight_(business)" title="White knight (business)">white knight</a>" strategy in 1974 during its attempts to defend Electric Storage Battery against a <a href="/wiki/Hostile_takeover" class="mw-redirect" title="Hostile takeover">hostile takeover</a> bid from International Nickel and Goldman's rival, <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This action improved the firm's reputation as an <a href="/wiki/Investment_advisor" class="mw-redirect" title="Investment advisor">investment advisor</a> because it pledged to no longer participate in hostile takeovers.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Weinberg" class="mw-redirect" title="John Weinberg">John L. Weinberg</a> (the son of Sidney Weinberg), and <a href="/wiki/John_C._Whitehead" title="John C. Whitehead">John C. Whitehead</a> assumed roles of co-senior partners in 1976, once again emphasizing the co-leadership at the firm. One of their initiatives was the establishment of 14 business principles that the firm still claims to apply.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_20th_century">Late 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Late 20th century" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On November 16, 1981, the firm acquired J. Aron &amp; Company, a <a href="/wiki/Commodities_trading" class="mw-redirect" title="Commodities trading">commodities trading</a> firm which merged with the Fixed Income division to become known as Fixed Income, Currencies, and Commodities. J. Aron was a player in the coffee and gold markets, and the former CEO of Goldman, <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Blankfein" title="Lloyd Blankfein">Lloyd Blankfein</a>, joined the firm as a result of this merger. In 1985 it underwrote the public offering of the <a href="/wiki/Real_estate_investment_trust" title="Real estate investment trust">real estate investment trust</a> that owned <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Center" title="Rockefeller Center">Rockefeller Center</a>, then the largest <a href="/wiki/Real_estate_investment_trust" title="Real estate investment trust">REIT</a> offering in history. In accordance with the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolution of the Soviet Union</a>, the firm also became involved in facilitating the global privatization movement by advising companies that were spinning off from their parent governments.
</p><p>In 1986, the firm formed Goldman Sachs Asset Management, which manages the majority of its mutual funds and <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge funds</a> today. In the same year, the firm also underwrote the IPO of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>, advised <a href="/wiki/GE" class="mw-redirect" title="GE">General Electric</a> on its acquisition of <a href="/wiki/RCA" title="RCA">RCA</a> and joined the <a href="/wiki/London_Stock_Exchange" title="London Stock Exchange">London</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_stock_exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="Tokyo stock exchange">Tokyo stock exchanges</a>. 1986 also was the year when Goldman became the first United States bank to rank in the top 10 of <a href="/wiki/Mergers_and_acquisitions" title="Mergers and acquisitions">mergers and acquisitions</a> in the United Kingdom. During the 1980s the firm became the first bank to distribute its investment research electronically and created the first public offering of original issue deep-discount <a href="/wiki/Bond_(finance)" title="Bond (finance)">bond</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Rubin" title="Robert Rubin">Robert Rubin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Friedman_(PFIAB)" title="Stephen Friedman (PFIAB)">Stephen Friedman</a> assumed the Co-Senior Partnership in 1990 and pledged to focus on globalization of the firm and strengthening the Merger &amp; Acquisition and Trading business lines. During their reign, the firm introduced paperless trading to the New York Stock Exchange and lead-managed the first-ever global <a href="/wiki/Corporate_bond" title="Corporate bond">debt offering</a> by a U.S. corporation. It also launched the <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs_Commodity_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="Goldman Sachs Commodity Index">Goldman Sachs Commodity Index</a> (GSCI) and opened a Beijing office in 1994. Also in 1994, <a href="/wiki/Jon_Corzine" title="Jon Corzine">Jon Corzine</a> assumed leadership of the firm as CEO, following the departure of Rubin and Friedman.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes1994September14RamirezAnthony_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes1994September14RamirezAnthony-25">[25]</a></sup> Rubin drew criticism in Congress for using a Treasury Department account under his personal control to distribute $20 billion to bail out Mexican bonds, of which Goldman was a key distributor.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes1995March2BradsherKeith_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes1995March2BradsherKeith-26">[26]</a></sup> On November 22, 1994, the Mexican Bolsa stock market had admitted Goldman Sachs and one other firm to operate on that market.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes1994November22_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes1994November22-27">[27]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1994_economic_crisis_in_Mexico" class="mw-redirect" title="1994 economic crisis in Mexico">1994 economic crisis in Mexico</a> threatened to wipe out the value of Mexico's bonds held by Goldman Sachs.
</p><p>In 1994, Goldman financed <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Center" title="Rockefeller Center">Rockefeller Center</a> in a deal that allowed it to take an ownership interest<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes1994November18HansellSaul_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes1994November18HansellSaul-28">[28]</a></sup> in 1996, and sold Rockefeller Center to <a href="/wiki/Tishman_Speyer" title="Tishman Speyer">Tishman Speyer</a> in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In April 1996, Goldman was lead underwriter of the <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a> IPO.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes1996April6_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes1996April6-30">[30]</a></sup> In 1998 it was the co-lead manager of the 2 trillion yen <a href="/wiki/NTT_DoCoMo" class="mw-redirect" title="NTT DoCoMo">NTT DoCoMo</a> IPO.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In 1999, Goldman acquired <a href="/wiki/Hull_Trading_Company" title="Hull Trading Company">Hull Trading Company</a>, one of the world's premier market-making firms, for $531 million.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes1999July13KahnJoseph_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes1999July13KahnJoseph-33">[33]</a></sup> After decades of debate among the partners, the company became a <a href="/wiki/Public_company" title="Public company">public company</a> via an <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offering</a> in May 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Goldman sold 12.6% of the company to the public, and, after the IPO, 48.3% of the company was held by the 221 former partners, 21.2% of the company was held by non-partner employees, and the remaining 17.9% was held by retired Goldman partners and two long-time investors, <a href="/wiki/Sumitomo_Bank_Ltd." class="mw-redirect" title="Sumitomo Bank Ltd.">Sumitomo Bank Ltd.</a> and Assn, the investing arm of <a href="/wiki/Kamehameha_Schools" title="Kamehameha Schools">Kamehameha Schools</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The shares were priced at $53 each and, after the IPO, Henry Paulson became Chairman and Chief Executive Officer, succeeding Jon Corzine.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 21st century" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Goldman Sachs purchased Spear, Leeds, &amp; Kellogg, one of the largest specialist firms on the New York Stock Exchange, for $6.3 billion in September 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2000September12McGeehanPatrick_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2000September12McGeehanPatrick-37">[37]</a></sup> In January 2000, Goldman, along with <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a>, was the lead manager for the first internet bond offering for the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In March 2003, the firm took a 45% stake in a joint venture with JBWere, the Australian investment bank.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In April 2003, Goldman acquired The Ayco Company L.P., a fee-based financial counseling service.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In December 2005, four years after its report on the emerging "<a href="/wiki/BRIC" title="BRIC">BRIC</a>" economies (Brazil, Russia, India, and China), Goldman Sachs named its "<a href="/wiki/Jim_O%27Neill,_Baron_O%27Neill_of_Gatley#Next_Eleven" title="Jim O'Neill, Baron O'Neill of Gatley">Next Eleven</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> list of countries, using macroeconomic stability, political maturity, openness of trade and investment policies and quality of education as criteria: <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p><p>In May 2006, Paulson left the firm to serve as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">United States Secretary of the Treasury</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_C._Blankfein" class="mw-redirect" title="Lloyd C. Blankfein">Lloyd C. Blankfein</a> was promoted to Chairman and Chief Executive Officer. In January 2007, Goldman, along with <a href="/wiki/CanWest_Global_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="CanWest Global Communications">CanWest Global Communications</a>, acquired <a href="/wiki/Alliance_Atlantis" title="Alliance Atlantis">Alliance Atlantis</a>, the company with the broadcast rights to the <a href="/wiki/CSI_(franchise)" title="CSI (franchise)">CSI franchise</a>.
</p><p>On September 10, 2018, Goldman Sachs acquired Boyd Corporation for a Leveraged Buyout of <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">$</a>3 billion.
</p><p>On May 16, 2019, Goldman Sachs acquired <a href="/wiki/United_Capital" class="mw-redirect" title="United Capital">United Capital</a> Financial Advisers, LLC for $750 million cash.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>Goldman Sachs will, among others, rely on CoreCard, a card management software owned by the Fintech company Intelligent Systems Corporation.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Subprime_mortgage_crisis:_2007.E2.80.932008"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Subprime_mortgage_crisis:_2007–2008">Subprime mortgage crisis: 2007–2008</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Subprime mortgage crisis: 2007–2008" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>During the 2007 <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">subprime mortgage crisis</a>, Goldman profited from the collapse in subprime mortgage bonds in summer 2007 by <a href="/wiki/Short_(finance)" title="Short (finance)">short-selling</a> subprime <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a>. Two Goldman traders, Michael Swenson and <a href="/wiki/Josh_Birnbaum" title="Josh Birnbaum">Josh Birnbaum</a>, are credited with being responsible for the firm's large profits during the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-FinancialTimes2010December10AllowayTracy_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinancialTimes2010December10AllowayTracy-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The pair, members of Goldman's <a href="/wiki/Structured_product" title="Structured product">structured products</a> group in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, made a profit of $4 billion by "betting" on a collapse in the subprime market and shorting mortgage-related securities. By summer 2007, they persuaded colleagues to see their point of view and convinced skeptical risk management executives.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-46">[46]</a></sup> The firm initially avoided large subprime write-downs and achieved a net profit due to significant losses on non-prime <a href="/wiki/Securitization" title="Securitization">securitized</a> loans being offset by gains on short mortgage positions. The firm's viability was later called into question as the crisis intensified in September 2008.
</p><p>On October 15, 2007, as the crisis had begun to unravel, <a href="/wiki/Allan_Sloan" title="Allan Sloan">Allan Sloan</a>, a senior editor for <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> magazine, wrote:<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote><p>So let's reduce this macro story to human scale. Meet GSAMP Trust 2006-S3, a $494 million drop in the junk-mortgage bucket, part of the more than half-a-trillion dollars of mortgage-backed securities issued last year. We found this issue by asking mortgage mavens to pick the worst deal they knew of that had been floated by a top-tier firm - and this one's pretty bad.
</p><p>It was sold by Goldman Sachs - GSAMP originally stood for Goldman Sachs Alternative Mortgage Products but now has become a name itself, like <a href="/wiki/AT%26T_Inc." class="mw-redirect" title="AT&amp;T Inc.">AT&amp;T</a> and <a href="/wiki/3M" title="3M">3M</a>.
</p><p>
This issue, which is backed by ultra-risky second-mortgage loans, contains all the elements that facilitated the housing bubble and bust. It's got speculators searching for quick gains in hot housing markets; it's got loans that seem to have been made with little or no serious analysis by lenders; and finally, it's got Wall Street, which churned out mortgage "product" because buyers wanted it. As they say on the Street, "When the ducks quack, feed them."</p></blockquote>
<p>On September 21, 2008, Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley, the last two major investment banks in the United States, both confirmed that they would become traditional <a href="/wiki/Bank_holding_company" title="Bank holding company">bank holding companies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2008September21HallJessica_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2008September21HallJessica-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The Federal Reserve's approval of their bid to become banks ended the business model of an independent securities firm, 75 years after Congress separated them from deposit-taking lenders, and capped weeks of chaos that sent <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a> into bankruptcy and led to the rushed sale of <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" class="mw-redirect" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America_Corp." class="mw-redirect" title="Bank of America Corp.">Bank of America Corp.</a><sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2008September22HilsenrathJon_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2008September22HilsenrathJon-50">[50]</a></sup> On September 23, 2008, <a href="/wiki/Berkshire_Hathaway" title="Berkshire Hathaway">Berkshire Hathaway</a> agreed to purchase $5 billion in Goldman's preferred stock, and also received <a href="/wiki/Warrant_(finance)" title="Warrant (finance)">warrants</a> to buy another $5 billion in Goldman's <a href="/wiki/Common_stock" title="Common stock">common stock</a> within five years.<sup id="cite_ref-5billion_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5billion-51">[51]</a></sup> The company also raised $5 billion via a public offering of shares at $123 per share.<sup id="cite_ref-5billion_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5billion-51">[51]</a></sup> Goldman also received a $10 billion <a href="/wiki/Preferred_stock" title="Preferred stock">preferred stock</a> investment from the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Treasury" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Treasury">U.S. Treasury</a> in October 2008, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">Troubled Asset Relief Program</a> (TARP).<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p><p><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Cuomo" title="Andrew Cuomo">Andrew Cuomo</a>, then <a href="/wiki/New_York_Attorney_General" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Attorney General">New York Attorney General</a>, questioned Goldman's decision to pay 953 employees bonuses of at least $1 million each after it received TARP funds in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2009July30GrocerStephen_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2009July30GrocerStephen-53">[53]</a></sup> In that same period, however, CEO Lloyd Blankfein and six other senior executives opted to forgo bonuses, stating they believed it was the right thing to do, in light of "the fact that we are part of an industry that's directly associated with the ongoing economic distress".<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2008November16GiannoneJoseph_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2008November16GiannoneJoseph-54">[54]</a></sup> Cuomo called the move "appropriate and prudent", and urged the executives of other banks to follow the firm's lead and refuse bonus payments.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2008November16GiannoneJoseph_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2008November16GiannoneJoseph-54">[54]</a></sup> In June 2009, Goldman Sachs repaid the U.S. Treasury's TARP investment, with 23% interest (in the form of $318 million in <a href="/wiki/Preferred_stock" title="Preferred stock">preferred dividend</a> payments and $1.418 billion in warrant redemptions).<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> On March 18, 2011, Goldman Sachs acquired <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> approval to buy back Berkshire's preferred stock in Goldman.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In December 2009, Goldman announced that its top 30 executives would be paid year-end bonuses in restricted stock that they cannot sell for five years, with <a href="/wiki/Clawback" title="Clawback">clawback</a> provisions.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2010January10CraigSusanne_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2010January10CraigSusanne-58">[58]</a></sup></p><p>During the 2008 financial crisis, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> introduced a number of short-term credit and liquidity facilities to help stabilize markets. Some of the transactions under these facilities provided liquidity to institutions whose disorderly failure could have severely stressed an already fragile financial system.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Goldman Sachs was one of the heaviest users of these loan facilities, taking out many loans between March 18, 2008, and April 22, 2009. The <a href="/wiki/Primary_Dealer_Credit_Facility" title="Primary Dealer Credit Facility">Primary Dealer Credit Facility</a> (PDCF), the first Fed facility ever to provide overnight loans to investment banks, loaned Goldman Sachs a total of $589 billion against collateral such as corporate market instruments and <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Term_Securities_Lending_Facility" title="Term Securities Lending Facility">Term Securities Lending Facility</a> (TSLF), which allows primary dealers to borrow liquid Treasury securities for one month in exchange for less liquid collateral, loaned Goldman Sachs a total of $193 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Goldman Sachs's borrowings totaled $782 billion in hundreds of revolving transactions over these months.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The loans were fully repaid in accordance with the terms of the facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Reemergence:_2008.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reemergence:_2008–present">Reemergence: 2008–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reemergence: 2008–present" data-section="7" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>According to a 2009 BrandAsset Valuator survey taken of 17,000 people nationwide, the firm's reputation suffered in 2008 and 2009, and rival Morgan Stanley was respected more than Goldman Sachs, a reversal of the sentiment in 2006. Goldman refused to comment on the findings.<sup id="cite_ref-FinancialTimes2009August2FarrellGreg_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinancialTimes2009August2FarrellGreg-64">[64]</a></sup> In 2011, Goldman took full control of JBWere in a $1 billion buyout.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In 2011, Goldman Sachs shut down the Global Alpha fund, once the firm's largest hedge funds. The announcement followed a reported decline in fund balances to less than $1.7 billion in June 2011 from $11 billion in 2007. The decline was caused by investors withdrawing from the fund following earlier substantial market losses.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2011September16LaCapraLaurenTara_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2011September16LaCapraLaurenTara-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2011September16RappaportLiz_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2011September16RappaportLiz-67">[67]</a></sup> Global Alpha was at one point one of the largest hedge funds in the world, with over $12 billion under management.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> It was started in 1995 with $10 million.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2011September15_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2011September15-69">[69]</a></sup> In September 2013, Goldman Sachs Asset Management announced it had entered into an agreement with Deutsche Asset &amp; Wealth Management to acquire its stable value business, with total assets under supervision of $21.6 billion as of June 30, 2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2013, together with <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a>, Goldman led a $17 billion bond offering by <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple Inc.</a>, the largest corporate-bond deal in history<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2013April30BurneKaty_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2013April30BurneKaty-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-appleraises_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-appleraises-72">[72]</a></sup> and Apple's first since 1996. Goldman Sachs managed both of Apple's previous bond offerings in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-appleraises_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-appleraises-72">[72]</a></sup> Goldman Sachs was the lead underwriter for <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>'s initial public offering in 2013. At the time, Goldman's position as lead underwriter for Twitter was considered "one of the biggest tech prizes around".<sup id="cite_ref-FinancialTimes2013September13AllowayTracy_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinancialTimes2013September13AllowayTracy-73">[73]</a></sup> Goldman earned approximately $22.8 million in fees from the Twitter IPO; however, the chief economist and strategist at ZT Wealth said, "Goldman being the first name on the S-1 has little to do with fees. This is about Goldman rebalancing itself as a serious leader and competing with Morgan Stanley's dominant position in technology."<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p><p>In 2013, Goldman underwrote the $2.913 billion Grand Parkway System Toll Revenue Bond offering for the Houston, Texas area, one of the fastest growing areas in the United States. The bond will be repaid from toll revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In June 2013, Goldman Sachs purchased loan portfolio from Brisbane-based <a href="/wiki/Suncorp_Group" title="Suncorp Group">Suncorp Group</a>, one of Australia's largest banks and insurance companies. The <a href="/wiki/Australian_dollar" title="Australian dollar">A$</a>1.6 billion face amount loan portfolio was purchased for A$960 million.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2013June12KellyRoss_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2013June12KellyRoss-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2015, Goldman Sachs agreed to acquire <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_Co." class="mw-redirect" title="General Electric Co.">General Electric Co.</a>'s GE Capital Bank on-line deposit platform. Terms of the transaction were not disclosed, but the purchase includes US$8-billion of on-line deposits and another US$8-billion of brokered certificates of deposit. The purchase allows Goldman Sachs to access a stable and inexpensive pool of source of funding.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcus_by_Goldman_Sachs_Logo.svg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Marcus_by_Goldman_Sachs_Logo.svg/200px-Marcus_by_Goldman_Sachs_Logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="73" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="112" data-file-height="41"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 200px;height: 73px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Marcus_by_Goldman_Sachs_Logo.svg/200px-Marcus_by_Goldman_Sachs_Logo.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="200" data-height="73" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Marcus_by_Goldman_Sachs_Logo.svg/300px-Marcus_by_Goldman_Sachs_Logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Marcus_by_Goldman_Sachs_Logo.svg/400px-Marcus_by_Goldman_Sachs_Logo.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcus_by_Goldman_Sachs_Logo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Logo of Marcus by Goldman Sachs</div></div></div>
<p>In April 2016, Goldman Sachs launched a <a href="/wiki/Direct_bank" title="Direct bank">direct bank</a>, GS Bank.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In October 2016, Goldman Sachs Bank USA started offering no-fee <a href="/wiki/Unsecured_debt" title="Unsecured debt">personal loans</a> under the brand Marcus by Goldman Sachs.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In March 2016, Goldman Sachs agreed to acquire <a href="/wiki/Financial_technology" title="Financial technology">financial technology</a> startup <a href="/wiki/Whurley#Honest_Dollar_(2014-present)" title="Whurley">Honest Dollar</a>, a digital retirement savings tool founded by American entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Whurley" title="Whurley">whurley</a>, focused on helping small-business employees and self-employed workers obtain affordable retirement plans. Terms of the deal were not disclosed.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2016March14MoyerLiz_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2016March14MoyerLiz-82">[82]</a></sup> In May 2017, Goldman Sachs purchased $2.8 billion of <a href="/wiki/PDVSA" title="PDVSA">PDVSA</a> 2022 bonds from the <a href="/wiki/Central_Bank_of_Venezuela" title="Central Bank of Venezuela">Central Bank of Venezuela</a> during the <a href="/wiki/2017_Venezuelan_protests" title="2017 Venezuelan protests">2017 Venezuelan protests</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2017May28VyasKejal_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2017May28VyasKejal-83">[83]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2018, Goldman Sachs bought Clarity Money, a personal finance startup, to be added to its Marcus by Goldman Sachs roster. This acquisition is expected to add over 1 million customers to the Marcus business.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2019, Goldman Sachs joined <a href="/wiki/Wells_Fargo" title="Wells Fargo">Wells Fargo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nvidia" title="Nvidia">NVIDIA</a> and Nexus Venture Partners to invest in H2O.ai. Under the terms of the deal, Jade Mandel from Goldman Sachs will be joining the H2O.ai Board. <sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p><p>In February 2020, Goldman Sachs, along with Aquiline and World Innovation Lab, raised $51 million to invest in Unqork, a New York-based software company, to accelerate a global expansion and move into new industries beyond insurance and financial services.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(2)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_operations">Current operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Current operations" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-2 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-2">
<p>The company includes 4 business units, as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-reutersoverview_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutersoverview-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Investment_banking">Investment banking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Investment banking" data-section="9" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment banking</a> accounted for 21% of total company revenues.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">Investment banking</a> includes financial advisory (mergers and acquisitions, investitures, corporate defense activities, <a href="/wiki/Restructuring" title="Restructuring">restructuring</a>, and spin-offs) and underwriting (capital raises, public offerings, and <a href="/wiki/Private_placements" class="mw-redirect" title="Private placements">private placements</a> of equity and debt instruments).
</p><p>Goldman Sachs is one of the leading M&amp;A advisory firms, often topping the <a href="/wiki/Thomson_Financial_league_tables" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomson Financial league tables">Thomson Financial league tables</a> in sizes of transactions. The firm gained a reputation as a white knight in the mergers and acquisitions sector by advising clients on how to avoid unfriendly <a href="/wiki/Hostile_takeover" class="mw-redirect" title="Hostile takeover">hostile takeovers</a>. During the 1980s, Goldman Sachs was the only major investment bank with a strict policy against helping to initiate a hostile takeover, which increased the firm's reputation immensely among sitting management teams at the time.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_Markets">Global Markets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Global Markets" data-section="10" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2017, Global Markets accounted for 37% of revenues.
</p><p>The segment is divided into four divisions and includes Fixed Income (the trading of interest rate and <a href="/wiki/Credit_(finance)" class="mw-redirect" title="Credit (finance)">credit</a> products, <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insurance-Linked_Securities_(ILS)" title="Insurance-Linked Securities (ILS)">insurance-linked securities</a> and structured and derivative products), Currency and Commodities (the trading of currencies and commodities), Equities (the trading of equities, <a href="/wiki/Equity_derivative" title="Equity derivative">equity derivatives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structured_product" title="Structured product">structured products</a>, <a href="/wiki/Option_(finance)" title="Option (finance)">options</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures contracts</a>), and Principal Investments (merchant banking investments and funds). This segment consists of the revenues and profit gained from the Bank's trading activities, both on behalf of its clients (known as <a href="/wiki/Flow_trading" title="Flow trading">flow trading</a>) and for its own account (known as <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_trading" title="Proprietary trading">proprietary trading</a>).
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Asset_Management">Asset Management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Asset Management" data-section="11" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, asset management accounted for 18% of total company revenues.<sup id="cite_ref-reutersoverview_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutersoverview-87">[87]</a></sup></p><p>The Asset Management division provides investment advisory and financial planning services and offers investment products (primarily through <a href="/wiki/Separately_managed_account" title="Separately managed account">separately managed accounts</a> and commingled vehicles) across all major asset classes to a diverse group of institutions and individuals worldwide. The division provides <a href="/wiki/Clearing_(finance)" title="Clearing (finance)">clearing</a>, financing, <a href="/wiki/Custodian_bank" title="Custodian bank">custody</a>, securities lending, and reporting services to institutional clients, including hedge funds, mutual funds, and pension funds. The division generates revenues primarily in the form of spreads, or management and transaction fees.<sup id="cite_ref-reutersoverview_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reutersoverview-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Goldman_Sachs_Capital_Partners">Goldman Sachs Capital Partners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Goldman Sachs Capital Partners" data-section="12" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs_Capital_Partners" title="Goldman Sachs Capital Partners">Goldman Sachs Capital Partners</a></div>
<p>Goldman Sachs Capital Partners is the private equity arm of Goldman Sachs that invests on behalf of institutional clients. It has invested over $17 billion in the 20 years from 1986 to 2006. One of the most prominent funds is the GS Capital Partners V fund, which raised over $8.5 billion for investment.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> On April 23, 2007, Goldman closed new investment in GS Capital Partners VI with $20 billion in committed capital, including $11 billion from qualified institutional and high-net-worth clients and $9 billion from Goldman Sachs and its employees.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> In 2016, the company announced it will raise up to $8 billion for a new fund focused on corporate buyouts, its first new fund since the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007-2008" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007-2008">financial crisis of 2007-2008</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2016July21HoffmanLiz_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2016July21HoffmanLiz-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Consumer_.26_Wealth_Management"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumer_&amp;_Wealth_Management">Consumer &amp; Wealth Management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Consumer &amp; Wealth Management" data-section="13" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Consumer &amp; Wealth Management includes management and other fees, incentive fees and results from deposit-taking activities related to the firm’s wealth management business. It also includes the results from providing loans through the firm’s private bank, providing unsecured loans and accepting deposits through the firm’s digital platform, Marcus by Goldman Sachs, and providing credit cards. Consumer &amp; Wealth Management oversees Goldman Sachs Private Wealth, Ayco, and <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs_Personal_Financial_Management" title="Goldman Sachs Personal Financial Management">Goldman Sachs Personal Financial Management</a>.
</p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(3)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Philanthropy">Philanthropy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Philanthropy" data-section="14" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-3 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-3">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs_Foundation" title="Goldman Sachs Foundation">Goldman Sachs Foundation</a></div>
<p>According to its website, Goldman Sachs has committed in excess of $1.6 billion to philanthropic initiatives since 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-engagement_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-engagement-91">[91]</a></sup> Goldman Sachs reports its environmental and social performance in an annual report on <a href="/wiki/Corporate_social_responsibility" title="Corporate social responsibility">Corporate social responsibility</a> that follows the <a href="/wiki/Global_Reporting_Initiative" title="Global Reporting Initiative">Global Reporting Initiative</a> protocol.<sup id="cite_ref-esg_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esg-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (February 2019)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>The company offers a <a href="/wiki/Donor_advised_fund" class="mw-redirect" title="Donor advised fund">donor advised fund</a> (DAF) called Goldman Sachs Gives that donates to charitable organizations with an employee donation match of up to $20,000.<sup id="cite_ref-esg_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esg-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2016March3PickerLeslie_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2016March3PickerLeslie-93">[93]</a></sup> A 2019 investigation by <i>Sludge</i> of DAFs and <a href="/wiki/Hate_group" title="Hate group">hate groups</a> found that Goldman Sach's donor advised fund had not been used to fund any SPLC hate groups, but that the fund did not have any explicit policy preventing such donations.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(4)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies_and_legal_issues">Controversies and legal issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Controversies and legal issues" data-section="15" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-4 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-4">
<table class="box-Very_long plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-very_long" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may be <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Article_size" title="Wikipedia:Article size">too long</a> to read and navigate comfortably</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please consider <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Splitting" title="Wikipedia:Splitting">splitting</a> content into sub-articles, <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Summary_style" title="Wikipedia:Summary style">condensing</a> it, or adding <a href="/wiki/Help:Section#Subsections" title="Help:Section">subheadings</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">April 2018</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs_controversies" title="Goldman Sachs controversies">Goldman Sachs controversies</a></div>
<p>Goldman has been accused of an assortment of misdeeds, including a general decline in ethical standards,<sup id="cite_ref-topexec_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-topexec-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2012March14SmithGreg_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2012March14SmithGreg-96">[96]</a></sup> working with dictatorial regimes,<sup id="cite_ref-GS_Russia_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GS_Russia-97">[97]</a></sup> cozy relationships with the US federal government via a "<a href="/wiki/Revolving_door_(politics)" title="Revolving door (politics)">revolving door</a>" of former employees,<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2008October17CreswellJulie_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2008October17CreswellJulie-98">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Insider_trading" title="Insider trading">insider trading</a> by some of its traders,<sup id="cite_ref-james-npr_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-james-npr-99">[99]</a></sup> and driving up prices of <a href="/wiki/Commodity_market" title="Commodity market">commodities</a> through <a href="/wiki/Futures_exchange" title="Futures exchange">futures</a> speculation.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid-100">[100]</a></sup> Goldman has denied wrongdoing in these cases.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="CEO-to-worker_pay_ratio">CEO-to-worker pay ratio</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: CEO-to-worker pay ratio" data-section="16" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>For the first time in 2018, a new Securities and Exchange Commission rule mandated under the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial reform requires publicly traded companies to disclose how their CEOs are compensated in comparison with their employees. In public filings, companies have to disclose their “Pay Ratios,” or the CEO's compensation divided by the median employee's.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> According to SEC filings, The Goldman Sachs Group Inc. paid its CEO $21,995,266 in 2017. The average worker employed by The Goldman Sachs Group Inc. was paid $135,165 in 2017; thus marking a CEO-to-worker Pay Ratio of 163 to 1.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>As of April 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, steelmaker Nucor represented the median CEO-to-worker Pay Ratio from SEC filings with values of 133 to 1.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> <i>Bloomberg BusinessWeek</i> on May 2, 2013, found the ratio of CEO pay to the typical worker rose from about 20-to-1 in the 1950s to 120-to-1 in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_the_financial_crisis_of_2007-2008">Role in the financial crisis of 2007-2008</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Role in the financial crisis of 2007-2008" data-section="17" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Goldman has been criticized in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2008">financial crisis of 2007–2008</a>, where some alleged that it misled its investors and profited from the collapse of the mortgage market. That time in Goldman's history brought investigations from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">United States Department of Justice</a>, and a lawsuit from the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission</a><sup id="cite_ref-FCIR-p192_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCIR-p192-105">[105]</a></sup> that resulted in Goldman paying a $550 million settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2010July15_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2010July15-106">[106]</a></sup> Goldman Sachs was "excoriated by the press and the public"<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> despite the non-retail nature of its business that would normally have kept it out of the public eye.<sup id="cite_ref-Devils-TARP_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devils-TARP-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2010June11SalmonFelix_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2010June11SalmonFelix-109">[109]</a></sup> Visibility and antagonism came from the $12.9 billion Goldman received, more than any other firm, from AIG counterparty payments provided by the bailout of AIG, the $10 billion in <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">TARP</a> money it received from the government (though the firm paid this back to the government), and a record $11.4 billion set aside for employee bonuses in the first half of 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Devils-TARP_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devils-TARP-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harper_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harper-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2009July14BowleyGraham_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2009July14BowleyGraham-111">[111]</a></sup> While all the investment banks were scolded by congressional investigations, Goldman Sachs was subject to "a solo hearing in front of the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Subcommittee_on_Investigations#Wall_Street_and_the_Financial_Crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Permanent Subcommittee on Investigations">Senate Permanent Subcommitee on Investigations</a>" and a quite critical report.<sup id="cite_ref-Devils-TARP_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devils-TARP-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> In a widely publicized story in <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Matt_Taibbi" title="Matt Taibbi">Matt Taibbi</a> characterized Goldman Sachs as a "great vampire squid" sucking money instead of blood, allegedly engineering "every major market manipulation since the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> ... from tech stocks to high gas prices".<sup id="cite_ref-carney_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carney-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Taibbi_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taibbi-114">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zamansky_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zamansky-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2011December13RooseKevin_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2011December13RooseKevin-116">[116]</a></sup></p><p>In June 2009, after the firm repaid the TARP investment from the U.S. Treasury, Goldman made some of the largest bonus payments in its history due to its strong financial performance.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2010January10CraigSusanne_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2010January10CraigSusanne-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Cuomo" title="Andrew Cuomo">Andrew Cuomo</a>, then <a href="/wiki/New_York_Attorney_General" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Attorney General">New York Attorney General</a>, questioned Goldman's decision to pay 953 employees bonuses of at least $1 million each after it received TARP funds in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2009July30GrocerStephen_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2009July30GrocerStephen-53">[53]</a></sup> That same period, however, CEO Lloyd Blankfein and 6 other senior executives opted to forgo bonuses, stating they believed it was the right thing to do, in light of "the fact that we are part of an industry that's directly associated with the ongoing economic distress".<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2008November16GiannoneJoseph_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2008November16GiannoneJoseph-54">[54]</a></sup></p><p>Goldman Sachs maintained that its net exposure to AIG was 'not material', and that the firm was protected by <a href="/wiki/Hedge_(finance)" title="Hedge (finance)">hedges</a> (in the form of CDSs with other counterparties) and $7.5 billion of <a href="/wiki/Collateral_(finance)" title="Collateral (finance)">collateral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2009March20_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2009March20-118">[118]</a></sup> The firm stated the cost of these hedges to be over $100 million.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2009April14vanPraagLucas_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2009April14vanPraagLucas-119">[119]</a></sup> According to Goldman, both the collateral and CDSs would have protected the bank from incurring an economic loss in the event of an AIG bankruptcy (however, because AIG was bailed out and not allowed to fail, these hedges did not pay out).<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> CFO <a href="/wiki/David_Viniar" title="David Viniar">David Viniar</a> stated that profits related to AIG in the first quarter of 2009 "rounded to zero", and profits in December were not significant. He went on to say that he was "mystified" by the interest the government and investors have shown in the bank's trading relationship with AIG.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p><p>Some have said, incorrectly according to others,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> that Goldman Sachs received preferential treatment from the government by being the only Wall Street firm to have participated in the crucial September meetings at the New York Fed, which decided AIG's fate. Much of this has stemmed from an inaccurate but often quoted <i>New York Times</i> article.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2008September27MorgensonGretchen_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2008September27MorgensonGretchen-123">[123]</a></sup> The article was later corrected to state that Blankfein, CEO of Goldman Sachs, was "<i>one of</i> the Wall Street chief executives at the meeting". <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_L.P." title="Bloomberg L.P.">Bloomberg</a> has also reported that representatives from other firms were indeed present at the September AIG meetings.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Furthermore, Goldman Sachs CFO <a href="/wiki/David_Viniar" title="David Viniar">David Viniar</a> stated that CEO Blankfein had never "met" with his predecessor and then-US Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson to discuss AIG;<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> however, there were frequent phone calls between the two of them.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Paulson was not present at the September meetings at the New York Fed. <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a> was hired by the Federal Reserve to advise on the AIG bailout.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2008September16AndrewsEdmund_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2008September16AndrewsEdmund-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Sale_of_Dragon_Systems_to_Lernout_.26_Hauspie_despite_accounting_issues"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sale_of_Dragon_Systems_to_Lernout_&amp;_Hauspie_despite_accounting_issues">Sale of Dragon Systems to Lernout &amp; Hauspie despite accounting issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Sale of Dragon Systems to Lernout &amp; Hauspie despite accounting issues" data-section="18" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2000, Goldman Sachs advised <a href="/wiki/Dragon_NaturallySpeaking" title="Dragon NaturallySpeaking">Dragon Systems</a> on its sale to <a href="/wiki/Lernout_%26_Hauspie" title="Lernout &amp; Hauspie">Lernout &amp; Hauspie</a> of Belgium for $580 million in L&amp;H stock. L&amp;H later collapsed due to accounting fraud and its stock price declined significantly. Jim and Janet Baker, founders and together 50% owners of Dragon, filed a lawsuit against Goldman Sachs, alleging negligence, intentional and negligent misrepresentation, and breach of fiduciary duty since Goldman did not warn Dragon or the Bakers of the accounting problems of the acquirer, L&amp;H. On January 23, 2013, a federal jury rejected the Bakers' claims and found Goldman Sachs not liable to the Bakers.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2013January23McLaughlinTim_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2013January23McLaughlinTim-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Stock_price_manipulation">Stock price manipulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Stock price manipulation" data-section="19" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Goldman Sachs was charged for repeatedly issuing research reports with extremely inflated financial projections for <a href="/wiki/Exodus_Communications" title="Exodus Communications">Exodus Communications</a> and Goldman Sachs was accused of giving Exodus its highest stock rating even though Goldman knew Exodus did not deserve such a rating.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> On July 15, 2003, Goldman Sachs, <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a> were sued for artificially inflating the stock price of RSL Communications by issuing untrue or materially misleading statements in research analyst reports, and paid $3,380,000 for settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p><p>Goldman Sachs is accused of asking for <a href="/wiki/Kickback_(bribery)" title="Kickback (bribery)">kickback bribes</a> from institutional clients who made large profits <a href="/wiki/Flipping" title="Flipping">flipping</a> stocks which Goldman had intentionally undervalued in <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offerings</a> it was underwriting. Documents <a href="/wiki/Under_seal" title="Under seal">under seal</a> in a decade-long lawsuit concerning <a href="/wiki/EToys.com" title="EToys.com">eToys.com</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offering</a> (IPO) in 1999 but released accidentally to the <i>New York Times</i> show that IPOs managed by Goldman were underpriced and that Goldman asked clients able to profit from the prices to increase business with it. The clients willingly complied with these demands because they understood it was necessary in order to participate in further such undervalued IPOs.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013March10NoceraJoe_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013March10NoceraJoe-131">[131]</a></sup> Companies going public and their initial consumer stockholders are both defrauded by this practice.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2013March11SalmonFelix_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2013March11SalmonFelix-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_offshore_tax_havens">Use of offshore tax havens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Use of offshore tax havens" data-section="20" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>A 2016 report by <a href="/wiki/Citizens_for_Tax_Justice" title="Citizens for Tax Justice">Citizens for Tax Justice</a> stated that "Goldman Sachs reports having 987 subsidiaries in offshore tax havens, 537 of which are in the <a href="/wiki/Cayman_Islands" title="Cayman Islands">Cayman Islands</a>, despite not operating a single legitimate office in that country, according to its own website. The group officially holds $28.6 billion offshore."
