The report also noted several other major U.S. banks and companies use the same tax-avoidance tactics.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p><p>In 2008, Goldman Sachs had an effective tax rate of only 1%, down from 34% the year before, and its tax liability decreased to $14 million in 2008, compared to $6 billion in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2008December16WhiteBen_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2008December16WhiteBen-134">[134]</a></sup> Critics have argued that the reduction in Goldman Sachs's tax rate was achieved by shifting its earnings to subsidiaries in low or no-tax nations, such as the Cayman Islands.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Involvement_in_the_European_sovereign_debt_crisis">Involvement in the European sovereign debt crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Involvement in the European sovereign debt crisis" data-section="21" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Papademos_2011-11-11.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Lucas_Papademos_2011-11-11.jpg/170px-Lucas_Papademos_2011-11-11.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="943" data-file-height="1192"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 170px;height: 215px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Lucas_Papademos_2011-11-11.jpg/170px-Lucas_Papademos_2011-11-11.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="170" data-height="215" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Papademos_2011-11-11.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Former Prime Minister of Greece Lucas Papademos</div></div></div>
<p>Goldman is being criticized for its involvement in the 2010 <a href="/wiki/European_sovereign_debt_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="European sovereign debt crisis">European sovereign debt crisis</a>. Goldman Sachs is reported to have systematically helped the Greek government mask the true facts concerning its national debt between the years 1998 and 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> In September 2009, Goldman Sachs, among others, created a special <a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swap" title="Credit default swap">credit default swap</a> (CDS) index to cover the high risk of Greece's national debt.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> The interest-rates of Greek national bonds soared, leading the Greek economy very close to bankruptcy in 2010 and 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2011June19CastleStephen_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2011June19CastleStephen-138">[138]</a></sup></p><p>Ties between Goldman Sachs and European leadership positions were another source of controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-Foley_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Papademos" title="Lucas Papademos">Lucas Papademos</a>, Greece's former prime minister, ran the Central Bank of Greece at the time of the controversial derivatives deals with Goldman Sachs that enabled Greece to hide the size of its debt.<sup id="cite_ref-Foley_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Petros_Christodoulou" title="Petros Christodoulou">Petros Christodoulou</a>, General Manager of the Greek <a href="/wiki/Public_Debt_Management_Agency_(Greece)" title="Public Debt Management Agency (Greece)">Public Debt Management Agency</a> is a former employee of Goldman Sachs.<sup id="cite_ref-Foley_139-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mario_Monti" title="Mario Monti">Mario Monti</a>, Italy's former prime minister and finance minister, who headed the new government that took over after Berlusconi's resignation, is an international adviser to Goldman Sachs.<sup id="cite_ref-Foley_139-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Otmar_Issing" title="Otmar Issing">Otmar Issing</a>, former board member of the Bundesbank and the Executive Board of the European Bank also advised Goldman Sachs.<sup id="cite_ref-Foley_139-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mario_Draghi" title="Mario Draghi">Mario Draghi</a>, head of the <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">European Central Bank</a>, is the former managing director of Goldman Sachs International.<sup id="cite_ref-Foley_139-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_Borges_(economist)" class="mw-redirect" title="António Borges (economist)">António Borges</a>, Head of the European Department of the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> in 2010-2011 and responsible for most of enterprise <a href="/wiki/Privatization" title="Privatization">privatizations</a> in <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> since 2011, is the former Vice Chairman of Goldman Sachs International.<sup id="cite_ref-Foley_139-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foley-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Moedas" title="Carlos Moedas">Carlos Moedas</a>, a former Goldman Sachs employee, was the Secretary of State to the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Portugal" title="Prime Minister of Portugal">Prime Minister of Portugal</a> and Director of ESAME, the agency created to monitor and control the implementation of the structural reforms agreed by the government of <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Troika_(triumvirate)" class="mw-redirect" title="Troika (triumvirate)">troika</a> composed of the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>, the European Central Bank and the International Monetary Fund. <a href="/wiki/Peter_Sutherland" title="Peter Sutherland">Peter Sutherland</a>, former Attorney General of Ireland was a non-executive director of Goldman Sachs International.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Employee.27s_views"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Employee's_views">Employee's views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Employee's views" data-section="22" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In March 2012, Greg Smith, then-head of Goldman Sachs US equity derivatives sales business in <a href="/wiki/Europe,_the_Middle_East_and_Africa" title="Europe, the Middle East and Africa">Europe, the Middle East and Africa</a> (EMEA), resigned his position via a critical letter printed as an <a href="/wiki/Op-ed" title="Op-ed">op-ed</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>. In the letter, he attacked Goldman Sachs CEO and Chairman Lloyd Blankfein for losing touch with the company's culture, which he described as "the secret sauce that made this place great and allowed us to earn our clients' trust for 143 years". Smith said that advising clients "to do what I believe is right for them" was becoming increasingly unpopular. Instead there was a "toxic and destructive" environment in which "the interests of the client continue to be sidelined", senior management described clients as "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/muppet" class="extiw" title="wikt:muppet">muppets</a>" and colleagues callously talked about "ripping their clients off".<sup id="cite_ref-topexec_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-topexec-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2012March14SmithGreg_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2012March14SmithGreg-96">[96]</a></sup> In reply, Goldman Sachs said that "we will only be successful if our clients are successful", claiming "this fundamental truth lies at the heart of how we conduct ourselves", and that "we don't think [Smith's comments] reflect the way we run our business".<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Later that year, Smith published a book titled <i>Why I left Goldman Sachs</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2012October19StewartJames_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2012October19StewartJames-143">[143]</a></sup></p><p>According to research by <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> after the op-ed was printed, almost all the claims made in Smith's incendiary Op-Ed about Goldman Sachs turned out to be "curiously short" on evidence. <i>The New York Times</i> never issued a retraction or admitted to any error in judgment in initially publishing Smith's op-ed.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2012October19StewartJames_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2012October19StewartJames-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg/170px-Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="427" data-file-height="640"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 170px;height: 255px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg/170px-Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="170" data-height="255" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Steven_Mandis_photo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Steven Mandis</div></div></div>
<p>In 2014 a book by former Goldman portfolio manager <a href="/wiki/Steven_George_Mandis" class="mw-redirect" title="Steven George Mandis">Steven George Mandis</a> was published entitled <i>What Happened to Goldman Sachs: An Insider's Story of Organizational Drift and Its Unintended Consequences</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-HBR_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBR-145">[145]</a></sup> Mandis also has a PhD dissertation about Goldman at <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>. Mandis left in 2004 after working for the firm for 12 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Mandis_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandis-146">[146]</a></sup> In an interview, Mandis said, "You read about Goldman Sachs, and it's either the bank is the best or the bank is the worst, this is not one of those books - things are never black or white."<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013September30LattmanPeter_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013September30LattmanPeter-147">[147]</a></sup> According to Mandis, there was an "organizational drift" in the company's evolution.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013September30LattmanPeter_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013September30LattmanPeter-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_bias_lawsuit">Gender bias lawsuit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Gender bias lawsuit" data-section="23" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2010, two former female employees filed a lawsuit against Goldman Sachs for gender discrimination. Cristina Chen-Oster and Shanna Orlich claimed that the firm fostered an "uncorrected culture of sexual harassment and assault" causing women to either be "sexualized or ignored". The suit cited both cultural and pay discrimination including frequent client trips to strip clubs, client golf outings that excluded female employees, and the fact that female vice presidents made 21% less than their male counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> In March 2018, the judge ruled that the female employees may pursue their claims as a group in a class-action lawsuit against Goldman on gender bias, but the class action excludes their claim on sexual harassment.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2018March30StempelJonathan_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2018March30StempelJonathan-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Advice_to_short_California_bonds_underwritten_by_the_firm">Advice to short California bonds underwritten by the firm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Advice to short California bonds underwritten by the firm" data-section="24" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On November 11, 2008, the <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i> reported that Goldman Sachs had both earned $25 million from underwriting California bonds, and advised other clients to <a href="/wiki/Short_(finance)" title="Short (finance)">short</a> those bonds.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> While some journalists criticized the contradictory actions,<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> others pointed out that the opposite investment decisions undertaken by the underwriting side and the trading side of the bank were normal and in line with regulations regarding <a href="/wiki/Chinese_wall#Finance" title="Chinese wall">Chinese walls</a>, and in fact critics had demanded increased independence between underwriting and trading.<sup id="cite_ref-theatlantic_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theatlantic-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Personnel_.22revolving-door.22_with_U.S._government"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Personnel_"revolving-door"_with_U.S._government'>Personnel "revolving-door" with U.S. government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title='Edit section: Personnel "revolving-door" with U.S. government' data-section="25" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>During 2008 Goldman Sachs received criticism for an apparent <a href="/wiki/Revolving_door_(politics)" title="Revolving door (politics)">revolving door</a> relationship, in which its employees and consultants have moved in and out of high level U.S. Government positions, creating the potential for conflicts of interest. The large number of former Goldman Sachs employees in the US government has been jokingly referred to "Government Sachs".<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2008October17CreswellJulie_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2008October17CreswellJulie-98">[98]</a></sup> Former Treasury Secretary Paulson is a former CEO of Goldman Sachs. Additional controversy attended the selection of former Goldman Sachs <a href="/wiki/Lobbyist" class="mw-redirect" title="Lobbyist">lobbyist</a> <a href="/wiki/Mark_A._Patterson" title="Mark A. Patterson">Mark A. Patterson</a> as chief of staff to Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Geithner" title="Timothy Geithner">Timothy Geithner</a>, despite President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>'s campaign promise that he would limit the influence of lobbyists in his administration.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> In February 2011, the <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Examiner" title="Washington Examiner">Washington Examiner</a></i> reported that Goldman Sachs was "the company from which Obama raised the most money in 2008", and that its "CEO <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Blankfein" title="Lloyd Blankfein">Lloyd Blankfein</a> has visited the White House 10 times".<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Insider_trading_cases">Insider trading cases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Insider trading cases" data-section="26" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 1986, Goldman Sachs investment banker David Brown pleaded guilty to charges of passing inside information on a takeover deal that eventually was provided to <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Boesky" title="Ivan Boesky">Ivan Boesky</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-spreading_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spreading-155">[155]</a></sup> In 1989, <a href="/wiki/Robert_M._Freeman" title="Robert M. Freeman">Robert M. Freeman</a>, who was a senior Partner, who was the Head of Risk Arbitrage, and who was a protégé of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Rubin" title="Robert Rubin">Robert Rubin</a>, pleaded guilty to <a href="/wiki/Insider_trading" title="Insider trading">insider trading</a>, for his own account and for the firm's account.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Rajat_Gupta_insider_trading_case">Rajat Gupta insider trading case</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Rajat Gupta insider trading case" data-section="27" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Raj_Rajaratnam/Galleon_Group,_Anil_Kumar,_and_Rajat_Gupta_insider_trading_cases" title="Raj Rajaratnam/Galleon Group, Anil Kumar, and Rajat Gupta insider trading cases">Raj Rajaratnam/Galleon Group, Anil Kumar, and Rajat Gupta insider trading cases</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg/220px-Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="207" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3200" data-file-height="3006"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 207px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg/220px-Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="207" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rajat_Kumar_Gupta_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_Davos_2010_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rajat Gupta</div></div></div>
<p>In April 2010, Goldman director <a href="/wiki/Rajat_Gupta" title="Rajat Gupta">Rajat Gupta</a> was named in an insider-trading case. It was said Gupta had "tipped off a hedge-fund billionaire", <a href="/wiki/Raj_Rajaratnam" title="Raj Rajaratnam">Raj Rajaratnam</a> of <a href="/wiki/Galleon_Group" title="Galleon Group">Galleon Group</a>, about the $5 billion Berkshire Hathaway investment in Goldman during the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008#Impacts_on_financial_institutions" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2008">financial crisis of 2007-2008</a>. According to the report, Gupta had told Goldman the month before his involvement became public that he wouldn't seek re-election as a director.<sup id="cite_ref-james-npr_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-james-npr-99">[99]</a></sup> In early 2011, with the delayed Rajaratnam criminal trial about to begin,<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2011February10McCoolGrant_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2011February10McCoolGrant-157">[157]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Securities and Exchange Commission">United States Securities and Exchange Commission</a> (SEC) announced civil charges against Gupta covering the Berkshire investment as well as confidential quarterly earnings information from Goldman and <a href="/wiki/Procter_%26_Gamble" title="Procter &amp; Gamble">Procter &amp; Gamble</a> (P&amp;G). Gupta was board member at P&amp;G until voluntarily resigning the day of the SEC announcement, after the charges were announced. "Gupta was an investor in some of the Galleon hedge funds when he passed the information along, and he had other business interests with Rajaratnam that were potentially lucrative.... Rajaratnam used the information from Gupta to illegally profit in hedge fund trades.... The information on Goldman made Rajaratnam's funds $17 million richer.... The Procter &amp; Gamble data created illegal profits of more than $570,000 for Galleon funds managed by others, the SEC said." Gupta was said to have "vigorously denied the SEC accusations". He was also a board member of <a href="/wiki/AMR_Corporation" title="AMR Corporation">AMR Corporation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p><p>Gupta was convicted in June 2012 on <a href="/wiki/Insider_trading" title="Insider trading">insider trading</a> charges stemming from <a href="/wiki/Raj_Rajaratnam/Galleon_Group,_Anil_Kumar,_and_Rajat_Gupta_insider_trading_cases" title="Raj Rajaratnam/Galleon Group, Anil Kumar, and Rajat Gupta insider trading cases">Galleon Group case</a> on <a href="#Insider_trading_conviction">four criminal felony counts</a> of <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy_(crime)" class="mw-redirect" title="Conspiracy (crime)">conspiracy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Securities_fraud" title="Securities fraud">securities fraud</a>. He was sentenced in October 2012 to two years in prison, an additional year on supervised release and ordered to pay $5 million in fines.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Abacus_mortgage-backed_CDOs_and_.24550_million_settlement_.282010.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Abacus_mortgage-backed_CDOs_and_$550_million_settlement_(2010)">Abacus mortgage-backed CDOs and $550 million settlement (2010)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Abacus mortgage-backed CDOs and $550 million settlement (2010)" data-section="28" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p><span id="Abacus_CDO"></span>
</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch#CDO_controversies" class="mw-redirect" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch § CDO controversies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magnetar_Capital" title="Magnetar Capital">Magnetar Capital</a></div>
<p>Unlike many investors and investment bankers, Goldman Sachs anticipated the <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">subprime mortgage crisis</a> that developed in 2007-8.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2009December24MorgensonGretchen_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2009December24MorgensonGretchen-160">[160]</a></sup> Some of its traders became "bearish" on the housing boom beginning in 2004 and developed mortgage-related securities, originally intended to protect Goldman from investment losses in the housing market. In late 2006, Goldman management changed the firm's overall stance on the mortgage market from positive to negative. As the market began its downturn, Goldman "created even more of these securities", no longer just hedging or satisfying investor orders but, according to business journalist Gretchen Morgenson, "enabling it to pocket huge profits" from the mortgage defaults and that Goldman "used the C.D.O.'s to place unusually large negative bets that were not mainly for hedging purposes".<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2009December24MorgensonGretchen_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2009December24MorgensonGretchen-160">[160]</a></sup> Authors Bethany McLean and Joe Nocera stated that "the firm's later insistence that it was merely a 'market maker' in these transactions - implying that it had no stake in the economic performance of the securities it was selling to clients - became less true over time"-<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p><p>The investments were called <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_CDO" title="Synthetic CDO">synthetic CDOs</a> because unlike regular <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligations</a>, the principal and interest they paid out came not from mortgages or other loans, but from premiums to pay for insurance against mortgage defaults - the insurance known as "<a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swap" title="Credit default swap">credit default swaps</a>". Goldman and some other hedge funds held a "short" position in the securities, paying the premiums, while the investors (insurance companies, pension funds, etc.) receiving the premiums were the "long" position. The longs were responsible for paying the insurance "claim" to Goldman and any other shorts if the mortgages or other loans defaulted. Through April 2007, Goldman issued over 20 CDOs in its "Abacus" series worth a total of $10.9 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2010April16StoryLouise_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2010April16StoryLouise-162">[162]</a></sup> All together Goldman packaged, sold, and shorted a total of 47 synthetic CDOs, with an aggregate face value of $66 billion between July 1, 2004, and May 31, 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p><p>But while Goldman was praised for its foresight, some argued its bets against the securities it created gave it a vested interest in their failure. These securities performed very poorly for the long investors and by April 2010, at least US$5 billion worth of the securities either carried "junk" ratings, or had defaulted.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> One CDO examined by critics which Goldman bet against, but also sold to investors, was the $800 million Hudson Mezzanine CDO issued in 2006. In the Senate Permanent Subcommittee hearings, Goldman executives stated that the company was trying to remove subprime securities from its books. Unable to sell them directly, it included them in the underlying securities of the CDO and took the short side, but critics McLean and Nocera complained the CDO prospectus did not explain this but described its contents as "'assets sourced from the Street', making it sound as though Goldman randomly selected the securities, instead of specifically creating a hedge for its own book".<sup id="cite_ref-devils-p274-1_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-devils-p274-1-165">[165]</a></sup> The CDO did not perform well, and by March 2008 - just 18 months after its issue - so many borrowers had defaulted that holders of the security paid out "about US$310 million to Goldman and others who had bet against it".<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2009December24MorgensonGretchen_160-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2009December24MorgensonGretchen-160">[160]</a></sup> Goldman's head of European fixed-income sales lamented in an e-mail made public by the Senate Permanent Subcommittee on Investigations, the "real bad feeling across European sales about some of the trades we did with clients" who had invested in the CDO. "The damage this has done to our franchise is very significant."<sup id="cite_ref-devils-p274-2_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-devils-p274-2-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_SEC_civil_fraud_lawsuit">2010 SEC civil fraud lawsuit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: 2010 SEC civil fraud lawsuit" data-section="29" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>In April 2010, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission</a> (SEC) charged Goldman Sachs and one of its vice-presidents, Fabrice Tourre, with securities fraud. The SEC alleged that Goldman had told buyers of a <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_CDO" title="Synthetic CDO">synthetic CDO</a>, a type of investment, that the underlying assets in the investment had been picked by an independent CDO manager, ACA Management. In fact, <a href="/wiki/Paulson_%26_Co." title="Paulson &amp; Co.">Paulson &amp; Co.</a> a <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge fund</a> that wanted to bet against the investment had played a "significant role" in the selection,<sup id="cite_ref-FCIR-p192_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCIR-p192-105">[105]</a></sup> and the package of securities turned out to become "one of the worst-performing mortgage deals of the housing crisis" because "less than a year after the deal was completed, 100% of the bonds selected for Abacus had been downgraded".<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2010April20LucchettiAaron_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2010April20LucchettiAaron-167">[167]</a></sup></p><p>The particular synthetic CDO that the SEC's 2010 fraud suit charged Goldman with misleading investors with was called Abacus 2007-AC1. Unlike many of the Abacus securities, 2007-AC1 did not have Goldman Sachs as a short seller, in fact, Goldman Sachs lost money on the deal.<sup id="cite_ref-7pillars_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7pillars-168">[168]</a></sup> That position was taken by the customer (<a href="/wiki/John_Paulson" title="John Paulson">John Paulson</a>) who hired Goldman to issue the security (according to the SEC's complaint). Paulson and his employees selected 90 BBB-rated mortgage bonds<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2010April20LucchettiAaron_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2010April20LucchettiAaron-167">[167]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2010April16WilchinsDan_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2010April16WilchinsDan-169">[169]</a></sup> that they believed were most likely to lose value and so the best bet to buy insurance for.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2010July15_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2010July15-106">[106]</a></sup> Paulson and the manager of the CDO, ACA Management, worked on the portfolio of 90 bonds to be insured (ACA allegedly unaware of Paulson's short position), coming to an agreement in late February 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2010April16WilchinsDan_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2010April16WilchinsDan-169">[169]</a></sup> Paulson paid Goldman approximately US$15 million for its work in the deal.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Paulson ultimately made a US$1 billion profit from the short investments, the profits coming from the losses of the investors and their insurers. These were primarily <a href="/wiki/IKB_Deutsche_Industriebank" title="IKB Deutsche Industriebank">IKB Deutsche Industriebank</a> (US$150 million loss), and the investors and insurers of another US$900 million - ACA Financial Guaranty Corp,<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2010April19SalmonFelix_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2010April19SalmonFelix-171">[171]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/ABN_AMRO" title="ABN AMRO">ABN AMRO</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Bank_of_Scotland" title="Royal Bank of Scotland">Royal Bank of Scotland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sec21489_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sec21489-172">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2010April22ThomasLandon_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2010April22ThomasLandon-173">[173]</a></sup></p><p>The SEC alleged that Goldman "materially misstated and omitted facts in disclosure documents" about the financial security,<sup id="cite_ref-FCIR-p192_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCIR-p192-105">[105]</a></sup> including the fact that it had "permitted a client that was betting against the mortgage market [the hedge fund manager Paulson &amp; Co.] to heavily influence which mortgage securities to include in an investment portfolio, while telling other investors that the securities were selected by an independent, objective third party", ACA Management.<sup id="cite_ref-sec21489_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sec21489-172">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> The SEC further alleged that "Tourre also misled ACA into believing ... that Paulson's interests in the collateral section [sic] process were aligned with ACA's, when, in reality, Paulson's interests were sharply conflicting".<sup id="cite_ref-sec21489_172-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sec21489-172">[172]</a></sup></p><p>In reply, Goldman issued a statement saying the SEC's charges were "unfounded in law and fact", and in later statements maintained that it had not structured the portfolio to lose money,<sup id="cite_ref-Goldman-Media_Release_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman-Media_Release-175">[175]</a></sup> that it had provided extensive disclosure to the long investors in the CDO, that it had lost $90 million, that ACA selected the portfolio without Goldman suggesting Paulson was to be a long investor, that it did not disclose the identities of a buyer to a seller, and vice versa, as it was not normal business practice for a market maker,<sup id="cite_ref-Goldman-Media_Release_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman-Media_Release-175">[175]</a></sup> and that ACA was itself the largest purchaser of the Abacus pool, investing US$951 million. Goldman also stated that any investor losses resulted from the overall negative performance of the entire sector, rather than from a particular security in the CDO.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldman-Media_Release_175-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman-Media_Release-175">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2010April19CorkeryMichael_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2010April19CorkeryMichael-176">[176]</a></sup> While some journalists and analysts have called these statements misleading,<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2010April19SalmonFelix_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2010April19SalmonFelix-171">[171]</a></sup> others believed Goldman's defense was strong and the SEC's case was weak.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup></p><p>Some experts on securities law such as <a href="/wiki/Duke_University_School_of_Law" title="Duke University School of Law">Duke University</a> law professor James Cox, believed the suit had merit because Goldman was aware of the relevance of Paulson's involvement and took steps to downplay it. Others, including <a href="/wiki/Wayne_State_University_Law_School" title="Wayne State University Law School">Wayne State University Law School</a> law professor Peter Henning, noted that the major purchasers were sophisticated investors capable of accurately assessing the risks involved, even without knowledge of the part played by Paulson.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2010April19JonesAshby_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2010April19JonesAshby-180">[180]</a></sup></p><p>Critics of Goldman Sachs point out that Paulson went to Goldman Sachs after being turned down for ethical reasons by another investment bank, <a href="/wiki/Bear_Stearns" title="Bear Stearns">Bear Stearns</a> who he had asked to build a CDO. Ira Wagner, the head of Bear Stearns's CDO Group in 2007, told the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Crisis_Inquiry_Commission" title="Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission">Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission</a> that having the short investors select the referenced collateral as a serious conflict of interest and the structure of the deal Paulson was proposing encouraged Paulson to pick the worst assets.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fiderer_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fiderer-182">[182]</a></sup> Describing Bear Stearns's reasoning, one author compared the deal to "a bettor asking a football owner to bench a star quarterback to improve the odds of his wager against the team".<sup id="cite_ref-Zuckerman_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuckerman-183">[183]</a></sup> Goldman claimed it lost $90 million, critics maintain it was simply unable (not due to a lack of trying) to shed its position before the underlying securities defaulted.<sup id="cite_ref-7pillars_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7pillars-168">[168]</a></sup></p><p>Critics also question whether the deal was ethical, even if it was legal.<sup id="cite_ref-devils-ethics-2_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-devils-ethics-2-184">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-chron_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chron-185">[185]</a></sup> Goldman had considerable advantages over its long customers. According to McLean and Nocera, there were dozens of securities being insured in the CDO - for example, another ABACUS<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> - had 130 credits from several different mortgage originators, commercial mortgage-backed securities, debt from Sallie Mae, credit cards, etc. Goldman bought mortgages to create securities, which made it "far more likely than its clients to have early knowledge" that the housing bubble was deflating and the mortgage originators like <a href="/wiki/New_Century" title="New Century">New Century</a> had begun to falsify documentation and sell mortgages to customers unable to pay the mortgage-holders back<sup id="cite_ref-McLean-272_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLean-272-187">[187]</a></sup> - which is why the fine print on at least one ABACUS prospectus warned long investors that the 'Protection Buyer' (Goldman) 'may have information, including material, non-public information' which it was not providing to the long investors.<sup id="cite_ref-McLean-272_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLean-272-187">[187]</a></sup></p><p>According to an article in the <i><a href="/wiki/Houston_Chronicle" title="Houston Chronicle">Houston Chronicle</a></i>, critics also worried that Abacus might undermine the position of the US "as a safe harbor for the world's investors" and that "The involvement of European interests as losers in this allegedly fixed game has attracted the attention of that region's political leaders, most notably British Prime Minister Gordon Brown, who has accused Goldman of "moral bankruptcy". This is, in short, a big global story ... Is what Goldman Sachs did with its Abacus investment vehicle illegal? That will be for the courts to decide, ... But it doesn't take a judge and jury to conclude that, legalities aside, this was just wrong."<sup id="cite_ref-chron_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chron-185">[185]</a></sup> On July 15, 2010, Goldman settled out of court, agreeing to pay the SEC and investors US$550 million, including $300 million to the U.S. government and $250 million to investors, one of the largest penalties ever paid by a Wall Street firm.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2010July15_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2010July15-106">[106]</a></sup> In August 2013, Tourre was found liable on 6 of 7 counts by a federal jury.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2013August1RaymondNate_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2013August1RaymondNate-188">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013August1CraigSusanne_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013August1CraigSusanne-189">[189]</a></sup> The company did not admit or deny wrongdoing, but did admit that its marketing materials for the investment "contained incomplete information", and agreed to change some of its business practices regarding mortgage investments.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2010July15_106-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2010July15-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourre_defense_of_ABACUS_lawsuit">Tourre defense of ABACUS lawsuit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Tourre defense of ABACUS lawsuit" data-section="30" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>The 2010 Goldman settlement did not cover charges against Goldman vice president and salesman for ABACUS, Fabrice Tourre.<sup id="cite_ref-7pillars_168-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7pillars-168">[168]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2010July15_106-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2010July15-106">[106]</a></sup> Tourre unsuccessfully sought a dismissal of the suit,<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2010September30StempelJonathan_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2010September30StempelJonathan-190">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2010September28KoppelNathan_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2010September28KoppelNathan-192">[192]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2010September29BrayChad_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2010September29BrayChad-193">[193]</a></sup> which then went to trial in 2013. On August 1, a federal jury found Tourre liable on six of seven counts, including that he misled investors about the mortgage deal. He was found not liable on the charge that he had deliberately made an untrue or misleading statement.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013August1CraigSusanne_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013August1CraigSusanne-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Alleged_commodity_price_manipulation">Alleged commodity price manipulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Alleged commodity price manipulation" data-section="31" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/2000s_commodities_boom" title="2000s commodities boom">2000s commodities boom</a></div>
<p>A provision of the 1999 financial deregulation law, the <a href="/wiki/Gramm-Leach-Bliley_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Gramm-Leach-Bliley Act">Gramm-Leach-Bliley Act</a>, allows commercial banks to enter into any business activity that is "complementary to a financial activity and does not pose a substantial risk to the safety or soundness of depository institutions or the financial system generally".<sup id="cite_ref-devious_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-devious-194">[194]</a></sup> In the years since the laws passing, Goldman Sachs and other investment banks (Morgan Stanley, JPMorgan Chase) have branched out into ownership of a wide variety of enterprises including raw materials, such as food products, zinc, copper, tin, nickel and, aluminum.
</p><p>Some critics, such as Matt Taibbi, believe that allowing a company to both "control the supply of crucial physical commodities, and also trade in the financial products that might be related to those markets", is "akin to letting casino owners who take book on NFL games during the week also coach all the teams on Sundays".<sup id="cite_ref-devious_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-devious-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Unauthorized_trades_by_Goldman_Sachs_trader_Matthew_Marshall_Taylor">Unauthorized trades by Goldman Sachs trader Matthew Marshall Taylor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Unauthorized trades by Goldman Sachs trader Matthew Marshall Taylor" data-section="32" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Former Goldman Sachs trader Matthew Marshall Taylor was convicted of hiding $8.3 billion worth of unauthorized trades involving derivatives on the <a href="/wiki/S%26P_500" class="mw-redirect" title="S&amp;P 500">S&amp;P 500</a> index by making "multiple false entries" into a Goldman trading system. When Goldman Sachs management uncovered the trades, Taylor was immediately fired. The trades cost the company $118 million, which Taylor was ordered to repay. In 2013, Taylor plead guilty to charges and was sentenced to 9 months in prison in addition to the monetary damages.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013December6AbramsRachel_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013December6AbramsRachel-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Goldman_Sachs_Commodity_Index_and_the_2005-2008_Food_Bubble">Goldman Sachs Commodity Index and the 2005-2008 Food Bubble</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Goldman Sachs Commodity Index and the 2005-2008 Food Bubble" data-section="33" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Frederick Kaufman, a contributing editor of <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Magazine" title="Harper's Magazine">Harper's Magazine</a></i>, argued in a 2010 article that Goldman's creation of the Goldman Sachs Commodity Index (now the <a href="/wiki/S%26P_GSCI" title="S&amp;P GSCI">S&amp;P GSCI</a>) helped passive investors such as pension funds, mutual funds and others engage in food price <a href="/wiki/Speculation" title="Speculation">speculation</a> by betting on financial products based on the commodity index. These financial products disturbed the normal relationship between <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply and demand</a>, making prices more volatile and defeating the price stabilization mechanism of the futures exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup></p><p>A June 2010 article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> defended commodity investors and oil index-tracking funds, citing a report by the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a> that found that commodities without futures markets and ignored by index-tracking funds also saw price rises during the period.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Aluminum_price_and_supply">Aluminum price and supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Aluminum price and supply" data-section="34" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>In a July 2013 article, <a href="/wiki/David_Kocieniewski" title="David Kocieniewski">David Kocieniewski</a>, a journalist with <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> accused Goldman Sachs and other Wall Street firms of "capitalizing on loosened federal regulations" to manipulate "a variety of commodities markets", particularly aluminum, citing "financial records, regulatory documents, and interviews with people involved in the activities".<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid-100">[100]</a></sup> After Goldman Sachs purchased aluminum warehousing company Metro International in 2010, the wait of warehouse customers for delivery of aluminum supplies to their factories - to make beer cans, home siding, and other products - went from an average of 6 weeks to more than 16 months, "according to industry records".<sup id="cite_ref-Zamansky_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zamansky-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid-100">[100]</a></sup> "Aluminum industry analysts say that the lengthy delays at Metro International after Goldman took over are a major reason the premium on all aluminum sold in the spot market has doubled since 2010."<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid_100-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid-100">[100]</a></sup> The price increase has cost "American consumers more than $5 billion" from 2010 to 2013 according to former industry executives, analysts and consultants.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid_100-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid-100">[100]</a></sup> The cause of this was alleged to be Goldman's ownership of a quarter of the national supply of aluminum - a million and a half tons - in network of 27 Metro International warehouses Goldman owns in Detroit, Michigan.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid_100-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stanley_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanley-200">[200]</a></sup> To avoid hoarding and price manipulation, the London Metal Exchange requires that "at least 3,000 tons of that metal must be moved out each day". Goldman has dealt with this requirement by moving the aluminum - not to factories, but "from one warehouse to another" - according to the Times.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid_100-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013July20KocieniewskiDavid-100">[100]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2013, Goldman Sachs was subpoenaed by the federal <a href="/wiki/Commodity_Futures_Trading_Commission" title="Commodity Futures Trading Commission">Commodity Futures Trading Commission</a> as part of an investigation into complaints that Goldman-owned metals warehouses had "intentionally created delays and inflated the price of aluminum".<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2013August12KocieniewskiDavid_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2013August12KocieniewskiDavid-201">[201]</a></sup> In December 2013, it was announced that 26 cases accusing Goldman Sachs and JPMorgan Chase, the two investment banks' warehousing businesses, and the London Metal Exchange in various combinations - of violating U.S. anti-trust laws, would be assigned to <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_Southern_District_of_New_York" title="United States District Court for the Southern District of New York">United States District Court for the Southern District of New York</a> Judge <a href="/wiki/Katherine_B._Forrest" title="Katherine B. Forrest">Katherine B. Forrest</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Harris_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-202">[202]</a></sup></p><p>According to Lydia DePillis of Wonkblog, when Goldman bought the warehouses it "started paying traders extra to bring their metal" to Goldman's warehouses "rather than anywhere else. The longer it stays, the more rent Goldman can charge, which is then passed on to the buyer in the form of a premium."<sup id="cite_ref-DePillis_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DePillis-203">[203]</a></sup> The effect is "amplified" by another company, <a href="/wiki/Glencore" title="Glencore">Glencore</a>, which is "doing the same thing in its warehouse in <a href="/wiki/Vlissingen" title="Vlissingen">Vlissingen</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-DePillis_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DePillis-203">[203]</a></sup></p><p>Michael DuVally, a spokesman for Goldman Sachs, said the cases are without merit.<sup id="cite_ref-Harris_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-202">[202]</a></sup> Columnist Matt Levine, writing for <i><a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_News" title="Bloomberg News">Bloomberg News</a></i>, described the conspiracy theory as "pretty silly", but said that it was a rational outcome of an irrational and inefficient system which Goldman Sachs may not have properly understood.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup></p><p>In December 2014, Goldman Sachs sold its aluminum warehousing business to Ruben Brothers.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2014December22BerthelsenChristian_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2014December22BerthelsenChristian-205">[205]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2014December22LeffJonathan_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2014December22LeffJonathan-206">[206]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2014December22PopperNathaniel_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2014December22PopperNathaniel-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_futures_speculation">Oil futures speculation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Oil futures speculation" data-section="35" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Investment banks, including Goldman, have also been accused of driving up the price of <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> by speculating on the oil <a href="/wiki/Futures_exchange" title="Futures exchange">futures exchange</a>. In August 2011, "confidential documents" were leaked "detailing the positions"<sup id="cite_ref-Sheppard_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard-208">[208]</a></sup> in the oil futures market of several investment banks, including Goldman Sachs, <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a>, <a href="/wiki/JPMorgan_Chase" title="JPMorgan Chase">JPMorgan Chase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Bank" title="Deutsche Bank">Deutsche Bank</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Barclays" title="Barclays">Barclays</a>, just before the peak in gasoline prices in the summer of 2008. The presence of positions by investment banks on the market was significant for the fact that the banks have deep pockets, and so the means to significantly sway prices, and unlike traditional market participants, neither produced oil nor ever took physical possession of actual barrels of oil they bought and sold. Journalist Kate Sheppard of <a href="/wiki/Mother_Jones_(magazine)" title="Mother Jones (magazine)">Mother Jones</a> called it "a development that many say is artificially raising the price of crude".<sup id="cite_ref-Sheppard_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard-208">[208]</a></sup> However, another source stated that, "Just before crude oil hit its record high in mid-2008, 15 of the world's largest banks were betting that prices would fall, according to private trading data..."<sup id="cite_ref-sanders_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sanders-209">[209]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2011, a couple of observers - Brad Johnson of the blog Climate Progress,<sup id="cite_ref-blame_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blame-210">[210]</a></sup> founded by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_J._Romm" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph J. Romm">Joseph J. Romm</a>, and Alain Sherter of <a href="/wiki/CBS_MoneyWatch" title="CBS MoneyWatch">CBS MoneyWatch</a><sup id="cite_ref-Sherter_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherter-211">[211]</a></sup> - noted that Goldman Sachs was warning investors of a dangerous spike in the price of oil. Climate Progress quoted Goldman as warning "that the price of oil has grown out of control due to excessive speculation" in petroleum futures, and that "net speculative positions are four times as high as in June 2008", when the price of oil peaked.<sup id="cite_ref-sanders_209-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sanders-209">[209]</a></sup></p><p>It stated that, "Goldman Sachs told its clients that it believed speculators like itself had artificially driven the price of oil at least $20 higher than supply and demand dictate."<sup id="cite_ref-blame_210-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blame-210">[210]</a></sup> Sherter noted that Goldman's concern over speculation did not prevent it (along with other speculators) from lobbying against regulations by the Commodity Futures Trading Commission to establish "position limits", which would cap the number of futures contracts a trader can hold, and thus prevent speculation.<sup id="cite_ref-Sherter_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherter-211">[211]</a></sup></p><p>
According to Joseph P. Kennedy II, by 2012, prices on the oil commodity market had become influenced by "hedge funds and bankers" pumping "billions of purely speculative dollars into commodity exchanges, chasing a limited number of barrels and driving up the price".<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2012April10KennedyJoseph_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2012April10KennedyJoseph-212">[212]</a></sup> The problem started, according to Kennedy, in 1991, when </p><blockquote><p>just a few years after oil futures began trading on the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mercantile_Exchange" title="New York Mercantile Exchange">New York Mercantile Exchange</a>, Goldman Sachs made an argument to the Commodity Futures Trading Commission that Wall Street dealers who put down big bets on oil should be considered legitimate hedgers and granted an exemption from regulatory limits on their trades.
The commission granted an exemption that ultimately allowed Goldman Sachs to process billions of dollars in speculative oil trades. Other exemptions followed,<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2012April10KennedyJoseph_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2012April10KennedyJoseph-212">[212]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p> and "by 2008, eight investment banks accounted for 32% of the total oil futures market".<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2012April10KennedyJoseph_212-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2012April10KennedyJoseph-212">[212]</a></sup></p><h3 class="in-block"><span id="Danish_utility_sale_.282014.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Danish_utility_sale_(2014)">Danish utility sale (2014)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Danish utility sale (2014)" data-section="36" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Goldman Sachs's purchase of an 18% stake in state-owned <a href="/wiki/DONG_Energy" class="mw-redirect" title="DONG Energy">DONG Energy</a> (now <a href="/wiki/%C3%98rsted_(company)" title="Ørsted (company)">Ørsted A/S</a><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup>) - <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>'s largest electric utility - set off a "political crisis" in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>. The sale - approved in January 30, 2014 - sparked protest in the form of the resignation of six cabinet ministers and the withdrawal of a party (<a href="/wiki/Socialist_People%27s_Party_(Denmark)" title="Socialist People's Party (Denmark)">Socialist People's Party</a>) from Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Helle_Thorning-Schmidt" title="Helle Thorning-Schmidt">Helle Thorning-Schmidt</a>'s leftist governing coalition.<sup id="cite_ref-Levring_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levring-214">[214]</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_Businessweek" title="Bloomberg Businessweek">Bloomberg Businessweek</a></i>, "the role of Goldman in the deal struck a nerve with the Danish public, which is still suffering from the after-effects of the global financial crisis". Protesters in <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a> gathered around a banner "with a drawing of a vampire squid - the description of Goldman used by Matt Taibbi in Rolling Stone in 2009".<sup id="cite_ref-Levring_214-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levring-214">[214]</a></sup> Opponents expressed concern that Goldman would have some say in DONG's management, and that Goldman planned to manage its investment through "subsidiaries in Luxembourg, the Cayman Islands, and Delaware, which made Danes suspicious that the bank would shift earnings to tax havens".<sup id="cite_ref-Levring_214-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levring-214">[214]</a></sup> Goldman purchased the 18% stake in 2014 for 8 billion kroner and sold just over a 6% stake in 2017 for 6.5 billion kroner.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Libya_investment_losses_.282013.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Libya_investment_losses_(2013)">Libya investment losses (2013)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Libya investment losses (2013)" data-section="37" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In January 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Libyan_Investment_Authority" title="Libyan Investment Authority">Libyan Investment Authority</a> (LIA) filed a lawsuit against Goldman for $1 billion after the firm lost 98% of the $1.3 billion the LIA invested with Goldman in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-Hodges_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hodges-216">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup></p><p>Goldman made more than $1 billion in derivatives trades with the LIA funds, which lost almost all their value but earned Goldman $350 million in profit.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes2014January30AndersonJenny_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes2014January30AndersonJenny-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> In court documents the firm has admitted to having used small gifts, occasional travel and an internship in order to gain access to Libya's sovereign wealth fund.<sup id="cite_ref-FinancialTimes2014September19BinhamCaroline_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinancialTimes2014September19BinhamCaroline-220">[220]</a></sup> In August 2014, Goldman dropped a bid to end the suit in a London court.<sup id="cite_ref-Hodges_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hodges-216">[216]</a></sup> In October 2016, after trial, the court entered a judgment in Goldman Sachs's favor.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Improper_securities_lending_practices">Improper securities lending practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Improper securities lending practices" data-section="38" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In January 2016, Goldman Sachs agreed to pay $15 million after it was found that a team of Goldman employees, between 2008 and 2013, "granted locates" by arranging to borrow securities to settle short sales without adequate review. However, U.S. regulation for short selling requires brokerages to enter an agreement to borrow securities on behalf of customers or to have "reasonable grounds" for believing that it can borrow the security before entering contracts to complete the sale. Additionally, Goldman Sachs gave "incomplete and unclear" responses to information requests from SEC compliance examiners in 2013 about the firm's securities lending practices.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2016January14BarlynSuzanne_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2016January14BarlynSuzanne-221">[221]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1MDB_Malaysian_sovereign_wealth_fund_scandal_.282015-present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1MDB_Malaysian_sovereign_wealth_fund_scandal_(2015-present)">1MDB Malaysian sovereign wealth fund scandal (2015-present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: 1MDB Malaysian sovereign wealth fund scandal (2015-present)" data-section="39" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1Malaysia_Development_Berhad_scandal" title="1Malaysia Development Berhad scandal">1Malaysia Development Berhad scandal</a></div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Thomson_Reuters_league_tables" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomson Reuters league tables">Thomson Reuters league tables</a>, Goldman Sachs was the most successful foreign investment bank in Malaysia from 2011 to 2013. In 2013, the bank had a 21% <a href="/wiki/Market_share" title="Market share">market share</a> in Malaysia's investment banking segment, double that of its nearest rival, mostly due to business with the Malaysian sovereign wealth fund, <a href="/wiki/1Malaysia_Development_Berhad" title="1Malaysia Development Berhad">1Malaysia Development Berhad</a> (1MDB).<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters2014January6KoswanageNiluksi_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters2014January6KoswanageNiluksi-222">[222]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></p><p>In 2015, U.S. prosecutors began examining the role of Goldman in helping 1MDB raise more than $6 billion. The 1MDB bond deals were said to generate "above-average" commissions and fees for Goldman amounting close to $600 million or more than 9% of the proceeds.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup></p><p>Beginning in 2016, Goldman was investigated for a $3 billion bond created by the bank for 1MDB. Prosecutors investigated whether the bank failed to comply with the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Bank_Secrecy" class="mw-redirect" title="Bank Secrecy">Bank Secrecy</a> Act, which requires financial institutions to report suspicious transactions to regulators.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2016June7BaerJustin_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2016June7BaerJustin-225">[225]</a></sup> In November 2018, Goldman's former chairman of Southeast Asia, Tim Leissner, admitted that more than US$200 million in proceeds from 1MDB bonds went into the accounts controlled by him and a relative.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> Leissner and another former Goldman banker, Roger Ng, together with Malaysian financier <a href="/wiki/Jho_Low" title="Jho Low">Jho Low</a> were charged with money laundering.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> Goldman chief executive David Solomon felt "horrible" about the ex-staff breaking the law by going around the policies<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> and apologized to Malaysians for Leissner’s role in the 1MDB scandal.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup></p><p>On December 17, 2018, Malaysia filed criminal charges against subsidiaries of Goldman and their former employees Leissner and Ng, alleging their commission of misleading statements in order to dishonestly misappropriate US$2.7 billion from the proceeds of 1MDB bonds arranged and underwritten by Goldman in 2012 and 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Purchase_Petr.C3.B3leos_de_Venezuela_bonds_.282017.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Purchase_Petróleos_de_Venezuela_bonds_(2017)">Purchase Petróleos de Venezuela bonds (2017)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Purchase Petróleos de Venezuela bonds (2017)" data-section="40" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In May 2017, Goldman Sachs purchased $2.8 billion of PDVSA 2022 bonds from the <a href="/wiki/Central_Bank_of_Venezuela" title="Central Bank of Venezuela">Central Bank of Venezuela</a> during the <a href="/wiki/2017_Venezuelan_protests" title="2017 Venezuelan protests">2017 Venezuelan protests</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ2017May28VyasKejal_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ2017May28VyasKejal-83">[83]</a></sup> when the country was suffering from malnutrition and hyperinflation.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-235">[235]</a></sup> In its original statement, Goldman stated that “We recognize that the situation is complex and evolving and that Venezuela is in crisis. We agree that life there has to get better, and we made the investment in part because we believe it will.”.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-236">[236]</a></sup> Venezuelan politicians and protesters in New York opposed to Maduro accused the bank of being of complicit of human rights abuses under the government and declared that the operation would fuel hunger in Venezuela by depriving the government of foreign exchange to import food, leading the securities to be dubbed “hunger bonds.”<sup id="cite_ref-:0_235-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-235">[235]</a></sup> The opposition-led <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Venezuela)" title="National Assembly (Venezuela)">National Assembly</a> voted to ask the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">U.S. Congress</a> to investigate the deal, which they called "immoral, opaque, and hypocritical given the socialist government’s anti-Wall Street rhetoric".<sup id="cite_ref-:1_236-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-236">[236]</a></sup></p><p>In a public letter to the bank’s chief executive, <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Blankfein" title="Lloyd Blankfein">Lloyd Blankfein</a>, the National Assembly president <a href="/wiki/Julio_Borges" title="Julio Borges">Julio Borges</a> said that “Goldman Sachs’s financial lifeline to the regime will serve to strengthen the brutal repression unleashed against the hundreds of thousands of Venezuelans peacefully protesting for political change in the country."<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> Sheila Patel, CEO of Goldman Sachs Asset Management’s international division, said in an interview that the incident "turned into a teachable moment within the firm" and "helped sharpen its focus on investing with an eye toward environmental, social and governance policies".<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Misreporting_transactions">Misreporting transactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Misreporting transactions" data-section="41" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In March 2019, Goldman Sachs was fined £34.4 million by the City (London) regulator for misreporting millions of transactions over a decade.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(5)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_contributions">Political contributions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Political contributions" data-section="42" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-5 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-5">
<p>Goldman Sachs employees have donated to both major American political parties, as well as candidates and <a href="/wiki/Super_PAC" class="mw-redirect" title="Super PAC">super PACs</a> belonging to both parties. According to the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Responsive_Politics" title="Center for Responsive Politics">Center for Responsive Politics</a>, Goldman Sachs and its employees collectively gave $4.7 million in the <a href="/wiki/2014_United_States_elections" title="2014 United States elections">2014 elections</a> to various candidates, <a href="/wiki/Leadership_PAC" class="mw-redirect" title="Leadership PAC">leadership PACs</a>, political parties, <a href="/wiki/527_group" class="mw-redirect" title="527 group">527 groups</a>, and outside spending entities.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> In the 2016 election cycle, Goldman employees were reported (as of September 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Goldman_Sachs&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>) to have donated $371,245 to the <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a> and $301,119 to the <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton_2016_presidential_campaign" title="Hillary Clinton 2016 presidential campaign">Hillary Clinton presidential campaign</a>. Goldman Sachs forbade its top level employees from donating to the <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump_2016_presidential_campaign" title="Donald Trump 2016 presidential campaign">Donald Trump presidential campaign</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CNNDonationsPolicy_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNDonationsPolicy-241">[241]</a></sup></p><p>In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Securities and Exchange Commission">Securities and Exchange Commission</a> issued regulations that limit asset managers' donations to state and local officials, and prohibit certain top-level employees from donating to such officials.<sup id="cite_ref-CNNDonationsPolicy_241-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNDonationsPolicy-241">[241]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MarketWatch_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarketWatch-242">[242]</a></sup> This SEC regulation is an anti-"pay-to-play" measure, intended to avoid the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_interest" title="Conflict of interest">conflict of interest</a>, or the appearance of a conflict of interest, as Goldman Sachs has business in managing state <a href="/wiki/Pension_fund" title="Pension fund">pension funds</a> and municipal debt.<sup id="cite_ref-CNNDonationsPolicy_241-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNDonationsPolicy-241">[241]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MarketWatch_242-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarketWatch-242">[242]</a></sup> In 2016, Goldman Sachs's compliance department barred the firm's 450 partners (its most senior employees) from making donations to state or local officials, as well as "any federal candidate who is a sitting state or local official".<sup id="cite_ref-CNNDonationsPolicy_241-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNDonationsPolicy-241">[241]</a></sup> One effect of this rule was to bar Goldman partners from directly donating to <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump_2016_presidential_campaign" title="Donald Trump 2016 presidential campaign">presidential campaign</a>, since Trump's running mate, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Pence" title="Mike Pence">Mike Pence</a>, was the sitting <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Indiana" title="Governor of Indiana">governor of Indiana</a>. Donations to <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton_2016_presidential_campaign" title="Hillary Clinton 2016 presidential campaign">presidential campaign</a> were not barred by the policy, since neither Clinton nor her running mate <a href="/wiki/Tim_Kaine" title="Tim Kaine">Tim Kaine</a> was a sitting state or local official.<sup id="cite_ref-CNNDonationsPolicy_241-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNDonationsPolicy-241">[241]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MarketWatch_242-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarketWatch-242">[242]</a></sup></p>
